Power Has No Effect: Distinguishing Agency from Capability
Sanusi concludes that power has no effect, distinguishes agency from power, and analyses the attribute of act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'arite theology denies that power (qudra) is the effective cause of actions, and how the attribute of act (al-fi'l) is analyzed to preserve God's free agency.

Core Idea
Power is not the cause; only the agent's act-attribute directly brings about existence.

In this final part of the tenth argument, Sanusi firmly separates the concepts of power and effective agency. He argues that if power itself produced effects, that would entail two independent causes—an impossibility. He then considers the divine attribute of 'act' (al-fi'l) and shows that it must be understood as specific to existence, making God a free agent rather than a necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
The passage begins by stating that power (al-qudra) has no effect on anything. Grasp why this is a significant claim in Islamic theology.
This contradicts common sense, where we think power leads to action.
2
Follow the argument against two independent causes
Sanusi argues that agency (al-fā'iliyya) and bringing-into-existence (al-mūjadiyya) are distinct from power, and they are the true causes. If power also caused the effect, two independent causes would concur, which is impossible.
This is a logical argument based on the impossibility of two sufficient causes for one effect.
3
Analyze the attribute of act (al-fi'l)
The passage then considers the attribute called 'act' or 'bringing-to-be'. If it is neutral like power, the same objections apply; if it is specific to existence, it becomes a necessary cause, not a free agent.
This preserves the Ash'arite view of God as a qādir mukhtār.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا ، امتنع أن يكون للقدرة أثر في الشيء . وذلك لأنه ثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الفاعلية والموجدية أمر مغاير للقدرة ، وثبت : أن المؤثر في وجود الأثر هو الفاعلية والموجدية . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون للقادرية أثر فيه ، وإلا لزم أن يجتمع على الأثر الواحد سببان مستقلان ، وهو محال . وأيضاً : فقد بينا أن القادرية ليس لها صلاحية التأثير البتة . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القادرية لا أثر لها البتة في وجود الأثر . وحينئذ لا تكون القدرة صالحة للتأثير البتة . وأما الصفة المسماة بالفعل والتكوين ، فإنها إن كانت صالحة لطرفي الفعل والترك ، كانت المباحث المذكورة في القدرة عائدة فيه ، وإن كانت متعينة لطرف الوجود وغير صالحة البتة لطرف العدم، كان ذلك موجباً بالذات ، لا قادراً متمكناً من الفعل والترك
And when this is established, it is impossible for power (al-qudra) to have an effect in the thing. That is because it is established by what we have mentioned that agency (al-fā'iliyya) and bringing-into-existence (al-mūjadiyya) are something distinct from power, and it is established that the effective agent in the existence of the effect is agency and bringing-into-existence. If this is so, it is impossible for capability (al-qādariyya) to have an effect in it, otherwise two independent causes would concur on one effect, which is impossible. Also, we have shown that capability absolutely has no capacity for effect. So it is established by what we have mentioned that capability has absolutely no effect in the existence of the effect. And then power is not fit for effect at all. As for the attribute called 'act' (al-fi'l) and 'bringing-to-be' (al-takwīn), if it is fit for both sides of action and refraining, then the aforementioned investigations concerning power would recur in it; and if it is specific to the side of existence and not at all fit for the side of non-existence, then that would be a necessary cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not a capable agent able to act or refrain.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage concludes the tenth argument by solidifying the distinction between power (qudra) and effective agency (fā'iliyya/mūjadiyya). The author reiterates that agency and bringing-into-existence are distinct from mere power, and that the true cause of an effect's existence is agency, not power. If power were also a cause, two independent causes would concur on one effect, which is impossible. Therefore, power has no effect whatsoever. The author then addresses a potential objection regarding the attribute called 'act' (al-fi'l) or 'bringing-to-be' (al-takwīn): if that attribute is neutral like power, the same problems arise; if it is specifically tied to existence, then it would be a necessary cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not a genuine capable agent who can choose to act or not. Thus, the argument preserves the Ash'arite conception of a qādir mukhtār (free agent) whose power does not directly necessitate the act.

قدرة (qudra)
فاعلية (fā'iliyya)
موجدية (mūjadiyya)
فعل (fi'l)
تكوين (takwīn)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi al-dhāt)
قادر مختار (qādir mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that adding 'act' as an attribute solves the problem of power's inefficacy.
Correction: This passage shows that either the attribute inherits the same issue (if neutral) or turns God into a necessary cause (if specific to existence), preserving the Ash'arite position.

Checkpoint

C) Because acts are eternal
D) Because power and act are identical

Key Takeaway
The true cause of an effect is the agent's effective agency (al-fā'iliyya), not mere power; the attribute of act must be specific to existence to preserve God's free agency.

The Eleventh Argument: Why Power Alone Cannot Cause an Act
Sanusi divides the possible cause of an act into three and refutes each except the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's tripartite division of the possible cause of an act and why only the third possibility (neither power nor its effect) can be correct after refuting objections.

Core Idea
The cause of an act cannot be power or its effect, but the agent's act of creation.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the eleventh argument, which systematically eliminates two common misconceptions about causality. First, he shows that if power itself were the cause, every effect would have to be eternal. Second, he shows that if an effect of power were the cause, an infinite regress would follow. He then addresses the objection that 'creation' might bypass these problems, showing that it merely restates the problem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the tripartite division
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi begins by dividing the possible cause (muqtaḍī) of an act into three: power, an effect of power, or neither. This is the framework for the entire argument.
Memorize the three options: (1) power itself, (2) effect issuing from power, (3) neither.
2
Refutation of the first option
Sanusi argues that if power itself were the cause, the act would have to occur whenever power exists. Since divine power is eternal, the act would be eternal, which is false for temporal acts. Understand why this is a reductio ad absurdum.
This assumes power is sufficient, not just necessary.
3
Refutation of the second option
If an effect of power were the cause, then the effect of power would itself need a cause. The same division applies, leading to an infinite regress. See why this is impossible.
The regress is vicious because no first cause is reached.
4
Analyze the third option and the objection
The third option is that the cause is neither power nor its effect. An objector says the act exists because the agent created it, not just because of power. Sanusi responds that 'creation' must be one of the three options, so the objection collapses back into the earlier refutations.
This shows that the third option is the only consistent position.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Review how Sanusi has eliminated two possibilities and defended the third. This argument supports the Ash'ari view that acts are directly created by God, not necessitated by power.
This connects to broader theological issues about free will and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشرة : إن المقتضي لحصول الفعل ، إما القدرة ، أو أثر يصدر عن القدرة ، أو لا القدرة ولا الأثر الصادر عنها . والأول : باطل . وإلا لزم حصول المقدور في جميع زمان القدرة ، فيلزم كون المقدور أزلياً ، لأجل كون القدرة أزلية . والثاني أيضاً : لأن التقسيم المذكور عائد في تأثير القدرة في ذلك الأثر ، فإن كان كذلك بواسطة أثر آخر ، لزم التسلسل وهو محال . وأما الثالث. فإنه يقتضي أن تكون القدرة ، وجميع آثارها : غير مؤثرة في حدوث هذا الأثر ، وذلك يقدح في كون القدرة : قدرة . فإن قالوا : هذا المقدور إنما وجد ، لأن القادر خلقه وأوجده ، لا لمجرد كونه قادراً قلنا : هذا باطل. لأن المفهوم من قولنا : خلقه : إما أن يكون هو نفس القدرة ، أو أثر صادر عنها ، أو شيء مغاير لهما . فيثبت : أن هذا السؤال غير وارد ، والله أعلم
The eleventh proof: The requisite (al-muqtaḍī) for the occurrence of the act is either power (al-qudra), or an effect (athar) issuing from power, or neither power nor the effect issuing from it. The first is false: otherwise the occurrence of the effect (al-maqdūr) would be necessary in all the time of power, and thus the effect would be eternal because power is eternal. The second is also false: because the aforementioned division would recur concerning the effect of power in that effect; if it is so through another effect, an infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would follow, which is impossible. As for the third: it requires that power and all its effects are not effective in the occurrence of this effect, and that impugns power being power. If they say: This effect exists only because the capable agent created it and brought it into existence, not merely because he is capable, we say: This is false, because the concept of 'He created it' is either power itself, or an effect issuing from it, or something distinct from both. So it is established that this objection is not valid. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh argument, which divides the possible cause (muqtaḍī) of an act into three exclusive possibilities: (1) power itself, (2) an effect issuing from power, or (3) something neither power nor its effect. The author refutes the first two. The first is impossible because if power were the cause, the effect would have to occur whenever power exists; since divine power is eternal, the effect would be eternal, which is false for temporal things. The second is also impossible because it would lead to an infinite regress: the effect of power would itself require a cause, which would be either another effect of power or the same, leading to either regress or circularity. The third option, that the cause is something else entirely, faces an objection: if someone says the effect exists because the agent created it (not just because of power), the author responds that 'creation' (khalq) itself is either identical to power, an effect of power, or something else. If it is identical to power, the first objection returns; if an effect of power, the second returns; if something else, the third option is simply restated without explanation. Thus, the objection is invalid. The argument shows that the only consistent position is that the true cause is not power per se, but the agent's act of creation, which is not reducible to power or its effect in the problematic sense.

Key Terms
مقتضي (muqtaḍī)
قدرة (qudra)
أثر (athar)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
خلق (khalq)
إيجاد (ījād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'creation' is simply another name for power, and thus the third option is not needed.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if 'creation' is invoked, it falls under one of the three options; if it is identical to power, the same problems persist.

Checkpoint
Why does the eleventh argument reject the possibility that the cause of an act is an effect issuing from power?
A) Because effects cannot be causes
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress regarding the power's effect on that effect
C) Because power has no effect at all
D) Because the effect is eternal

Key Takeaway
The only consistent position is that the act is caused directly by the agent's creation, not by power or its effects in a way that leads to eternalism or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your ability (power) is the direct cause of your actions, forgetting that it is Allah's creation that brings the act about? How does this affect your reliance on Him?
?
When you fail to accomplish something, do you blame lack of ability or do you recognize that the actual cause is Allah's will to create that action for you? How would your response change if you truly internalized this argument?
?
The argument shows that if power alone caused acts, acts would be eternal. Do you sometimes act as if your own power is independent and eternal, forgetting that each moment of ability is created by Allah? What one change can you make today to remind yourself that 'there is no power nor strength except by Allah'?

The Eleventh Proof: Trilemma on the Determinant of the Act
Sanusi presents the eleventh proof, a trilemma against the qadir's effect being via power or its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the eleventh proof, which uses a trilemma to show that the act of a capable agent cannot be causally determined by power, an effect of power, or anything else.

Core Idea
The act's determinant cannot be power, its effect, or neither—every option fails.

The author presents a compact logical argument that analyzes the causal determinant of an act. The argument is a trilemma: three exhaustive possibilities are considered, each shown to be impossible. This forces the conclusion that the act must be attributed to the agent in a way that avoids these difficulties. The proof also anticipates and refutes a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the trilemma's three branches.
2
Understand the trilemma
Identify the three possibilities: power, an effect of power, or neither. The author rejects all three.
The first leads to eternal effects, the second to infinite regress, the third nullifies power.
3
Examine the objection and response
Note the objection that 'created' is different. See how the author reduces it to the same trilemma.
4
Reflect on the proof's purpose
Ask yourself why this proof is important in the larger theological context. It defends divine power without implying temporal effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشرة : إن المقتضي لحصول الفعل ، إما القدرة ، أو أثر يصدر عن القدرة أو لا القدرة ، ولا الأثر الصادر عنها . والأول : باطل . وإلا لزم حصول المقدور في جميع زمان القدرة ، فيلزم كون المقدور أزلياً ، لأجل كون القدرة أزلية . والثاني أيضاً : لأن التقسيم المذكور عائد في تأثير القدرة في ذلك الأثر ، فإن كان كذلك بواسطة أثر آخر ، لزم التسلسل وهو محال . وأما الثالث. فإنه يقتضي أن تكون القدرة ، وجميع آثارها : غير مؤثرة في حدوث هذا الأثر ، وذلك يقدح في كون القدرة : قدرة . فإن قالوا : هذا المقدور إنما وجد ، لأن القادر خلقه وأوجده ، لا لمجرد كونه قادراً قلنا : هذا باطل. لأن المفهوم من قولنا : خلقه : إما أن يكون هو نفس القدرة ، أو أثر صادر عنها ، أو شيء مغاير لهما . فيثبت : أن هذا السؤال غير وارد ، والله أعلم
The eleventh proof: The determinant (muqtadi) for the occurrence of the act is either power, or an effect emanating from power, or neither power nor the effect emanating from it. The first is false, otherwise the effect would occur in all the time of power, necessitating that the effect be eternal because power is eternal. The second is also false, because the aforementioned division recurs concerning the influence of power on that effect; if it is through another effect, then a regress (tasalsul) follows, which is absurd. As for the third, it requires that power and all its effects be uninfluential in the occurrence of this effect, and that disparages power from being power. If they say: This effect only existed because the agent (qadir) created it and brought it into existence, not merely because it is powerful, we say: This is false, because the meaning of 'He created it' is either power itself, or an effect emanating from it, or something else besides the two. Thus it is established that this objection is not valid. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh logical proof (al-hujja al-hadiya 'ashara) against a certain conception of the 'capable agent' (al-qadir). The argument is structured as a trilemma: the determinant (muqtadi) that brings about the act must be one of three things: (1) power (al-qudra) itself, (2) some effect (athar) that emanates from power, or (3) neither power nor any effect of power. The author refutes each option in turn. The first option is false because if power itself were the direct cause, then the effect would always exist whenever power exists. Since power is eternal (azali) in the case of God, the effect would have to be eternal as well—but contingent things are temporal. The second option leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul): if an effect of power causes the act, then the same trilemma applies to that effect's own causal influence, and if that is again via another effect, the regress continues indefinitely, which is absurd. The third option renders power and all its effects completely irrelevant to the act's occurrence; but this undermines the very concept of being 'powerful' (qudra). The author then anticipates an objection: someone might argue that the act exists because the agent 'created' it (khalaqahu), not merely because he is powerful. The author responds by analyzing the term 'created': it must refer either to power itself, or an effect of power, or something else. In any case, the same three options recur, so the objection does not escape the trilemma. Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms
المقتضي
القدرة
المقدور
أزلي
التسلسل
خلقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'created' is a different type of causality that escapes the trilemma.
Correction: The author shows that 'created' must mean either power itself, an effect of power, or something else—all covered by the trilemma, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
In the eleventh proof, what is the consequence if power itself is the direct cause of the act?
A) Infinite regress
B) The act would be eternal
C) Power would be irrelevant
D) The act would not occur

Key Takeaway
The eleventh proof shows that the act's determinant cannot be power, an effect of power, or something else, thereby defending divine agency without compromising divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own ability to achieve something, do you ever consider that the real determinant might not be your power alone? How does this proof challenge the assumption that you are the true cause of your actions?
?
Think of a time you attributed an outcome entirely to your own effort. According to this trilemma, where does that belief lead logically? Are you willing to re-evaluate your understanding of causality and acknowledge that only God's power and will are the true determinants?
?
The proof shows that claiming 'God created it' does not escape the trilemma. How often do you use religious language to avoid deeper reflection? Will you now accept the full weight of the kalam argument and let it reshape your trust in God alone?

Equality of Times and Specification
First branch of Razi's fifth proof: times equal in quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of Razi's fifth proof: how the claim of a specific creation time collapses if that time is equal to all others in quiddity.

Core Idea
If all times are equal in essence, no will can rationally prefer one over another.

Razi begins his fifth proof by setting up a conditional: if the world were created, its creation must be at a specific time chosen by God. He then divides possibilities: either that time is equal in quiddity to all other times, or it is different. In this lesson, we examine the first branch and its devastating consequence for the claim of temporal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional premise
Read from the start of the proof: 'If the world were created...' Understand the conditional link between creation and specification.
This premise is shared with the earlier proofs.
2
Identify the two divisions
Razi divides the discussion into two cases: time equal in quiddity or different. Focus on the first case here.
3
Follow the equality argument
If times are equal in quiddity, they must be equal in all concomitants. Then the will's relation to them is equal, so it cannot prefer one. This contradicts the specification required by creation.
Notice the logical necessity of equality in concomitants.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument shows that if all times are equal, no rational specification is possible. Therefore, creation at a specific time cannot be true under this branch.
This is a reductio of the first possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجب القول بالقدم، وذلك يناقض ثبوت الحدوث. الحجة الخامسة: لو كان العالم حادثاً، لكان حدوثه بسبب أن الخالق [تعالى] خصص إحداثه بذلك الوقت [المعين] على ما تقدم تقرير هذه الشرطية، إلا أن ذلك محال. لأن ذلك الوقت إما أن يكون مساوياً لسائر الأوقات في تمام الماهية، أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول وجب كونها أيضاً متساوية في جميع اللوازم، لأن الأشياء المتساوية في تمام الماهية، يجب استواؤها في جميع لوازم الماهية. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كانت نسبة تلك الإرادة إلى أجزاء ذلك الوقت على السوية. وإذا وجب حصول هذا الإستواء، كان ذلك منافياً للقول بأن الإرادة يجب تعلقها بإحداث العالم في بعض تلك الأوقات دون البعض. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: الأجزاء المفترضة في ذلك الوقت: مختلفة بالماهية، متباينة بالحقيقة. فنقول: حصول تلك الأشياء، إما [أن تكون] بأنفسها وذواتها وإما أن تكون بإحداث [ذات] الله تعالى. والأول باطل، لأنه يقتضي حدوث الشيء لنفسه ولذاته، من غير محدث، وهو محال. وأيضاً: إذا فرضناها [حادثة] بأنفسها وذواتها من غير محدث البتة، ثم كانت ماهية كل واحد منها، مخالفة لماهية الأخرى، لم يمتنع جعل واحد منها، موجباً لأثر خاص. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه [لا يمتنع أن يكون المؤثر في حدوث حوادث هذا العالم، تلك الآنات المتلاصقة، وتلك الأجزاء المتعاقبة، وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه] يلزم نفي الصانع، وذلك باطل. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: إن حدوث تلك الآنات المتلاصقة، إنما كان بإحداث الله [تعالى] وبإيجاده، فحينئذ يعود السؤال إلى أنه: لم خصص إحداث كل واحد منها بكونه متقدماً على الآخر، أو متأخراً عنه؟ فإن كان كذلك، لأجل وقت آخر، عاد الطلب فيه، لزم التسلسل وهو محال. فيثبت: أن القول بأن الإرادة اقتضت تخصيص إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين، يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة، فيكون القول به باطلاً. وبهذا الدليل يظهر أن الذي يقال: إنه تعالى [وتقدس] خصص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت لكون ذلك الوقت مشتملاً على مصلحة خفية: قول باطل. لأنا بينا: أن الأوقات كلها متساوية في تمام الماهية [والله أعلم].
It necessitates the assertion of eternity, and that contradicts the establishment of creation. The fifth proof: If the world were created, its creation would be due to the Creator [Most High] specifying its creation at that [particular] time, as previously established in this conditional statement, except that that is impossible. Because that time either is equal to the rest of the times in complete quiddity, or it is not. If it is the first, then they must also be equal in all concomitants, because things equal in complete quiddity must be equal in all concomitants of the quiddity. And when that is the case, the relation of that will to the parts of that time is upon equality. And when this equality necessarily obtains, that contradicts the assertion that the will must attach to the creation of the world at some of those times to the exclusion of others. As for the second division: that it is said: the parts assumed in that time are different in quiddity, distinct in reality. We say: the occurrence of those things is either by themselves and their essences or by the creation of [the essence of] God Most High. The first is false, because it necessitates the occurrence of a thing by itself and its essence, without a creator, and that is impossible. Also: if we assume them [occurring] by themselves and their essences without any creator at all, and then the quiddity of each one of them differs from the quiddity of the other, it is not impossible to make one of them the cause of a specific effect. And on this assumption, it is [not impossible that the effect on the occurrence of the events of this world is those contiguous moments and those successive parts, and on this assumption] it leads to the denial of the Maker, and that is false. As for the second division: that it is said: the occurrence of those contiguous moments was only by the creation of God [Most High] and by His bringing into existence, then the question returns to: why did He specify each one of them being prior to the other or posterior to it? If that is so, because of another time, the request returns in it, and infinite regress follows, and that is impossible. Thus it is established: that the assertion that the will necessitated the specification of the world's creation at the particular time leads to these false divisions, so the assertion with it is false. And by this proof it becomes clear that what is said: that He Most High [and Exalted] specified the creation of the world at that time because that time contained a hidden benefit: is a false statement. Because we have shown: that all times are equal in complete quiddity [and God knows best].
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa) against the claim that the world is created (hadith) by a willing agent who chose a specific time for its creation. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if the world were created at a particular time, that time would have to be either equal in essence to all other times or different. If equal, then the divine will would have no reason to prefer that moment, violating the necessity of specification (takhsis). If different, then either the moments cause themselves—impossible—or they are created by God, but then the same question of why one moment precedes another recurs, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus both possibilities are impossible, proving that the claim of a created world with a specific time is false. The author also refutes the objection that the chosen time contains a hidden benefit, since all times are equal in quiddity. This proof aligns with the kalām cosmological argument for the eternity of the world (qidam) or at least refutes a specific version of temporal creation.

Key Terms
قدم
حدوث
حجة خامسة
ماهية
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first branch of Razi's fifth proof?
A) The time of creation is equal to all others.
B) If times are equal in quiddity, the will cannot specify one time.
C) Times must be different in quiddity.
D) The will must create at the earliest time.

Key Takeaway
If all times are equal in essence, no rational ground for specifying one time for creation exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that God's choice to create the world at a particular moment in time was arbitrary? After this lesson, do you realize that a rational specification requires a differentiating factor? How does that deepen your awe of God's wisdom?

Different Times Lead to Absurdities
Second branch of Razi's fifth proof: times different in quiddity, with two subarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second branch of Razi's fifth proof, where different quiddities lead to either self-causation or infinite regress, both impossible.

Core Idea
If times differ in essence, their occurrence must be either self-caused (impossible) or God-caused (leading to infinite regress).

Having disposed of the case where times are equal, Razi now considers the alternative: the parts of time are different in quiddity. This opens two sub-possibilities: either these moments cause themselves, or they are created by God. Both lead to absurdities—self-causation denies the need for a creator, and divine causation of each moment leads to an infinite regress of reasons. Thus, the claim of a specific creation time is refuted entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division
Read from 'As for the second division...' to the end of the passage. Note the two sub-cases: self-causation or divine causation.
2
Analyze the self-causation argument
Razi argues that if moments cause themselves, that implies an effect without a cause, which is impossible. Also, if each moment is self-caused, their differing quiddities could make each a cause of effects, which would eliminate the need for God.
Notice the argument against independence from God.
3
Follow the divine causation argument
If God creates each moment, we can ask why He makes one prior to another. If the answer is 'because of another time,' we get an infinite regress—impossible. So this option fails.
This is a classic infinite regress argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both possibilities lead to absurdities, so the initial assumption that the world was created at a specific time must be false. The hidden benefit objection is also refuted because all times are equal.
The proof concludes by showing the hidden benefit claim is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all moments are equal in essence before God, what does that mean for your own procrastination? Are you waiting for a 'better time' to obey or change, knowing that no time is inherently better than another? What is stopping you from acting now?
?
Razi shows that demanding a reason for God's timing leads to absurdities. Have you ever questioned God's wisdom because His timing didn't make sense to you? How does this proof challenge your tendency to want explanations before submitting? Will you trust His will even when you see no reason?
?
The hidden benefit objection is refuted because times are equal. Is there an area in your life where you assume that God's command must have a hidden benefit for you to follow it? Can you obey without needing to see the benefit, simply because He is the Creator?

Objection and Answer: Is Eternal Creation Impossible?
Objection to the first proof and the author's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that eternal creation is impossible and how the author refutes it by redefining the role of eternity.

Core Idea
Eternal creation is not impossible; the objection misdefines eternity.

The objector argues that eternal creation is intrinsically impossible, so God's not doing it is no deficiency. The author responds by showing that the definition of eternity as a barrier is false, and even if it were, it was removed. This exchange clarifies the relationship between God's power and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objector's claim: that creation cannot be eternal because it requires a temporal beginning.
Notice that the objector also includes a second argument about God's power.
2
Identify the two main arguments in the objection
The objection has two parts: (1) eternal creation is impossible because it means bringing from non-existence, which needs a past, but eternity precludes that. (2) deficiency only follows if God were incapable, but He is not.
3
Read the author's responses
The author responds to each part: (1) The definition of eternity as a barrier is false; even if it were, it was removed before creation. (2) If eternity prevented action, then God would be weak, which is a greater deficiency.
The author turns the objection back on the objector.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the objection and answer strengthen the first proof. The objector's assumptions about eternity are shown to be flawed, and the proof stands.
This lesson connects to the broader topic of God's power and creation in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : [ يلزم ] على الحجة الأولى : ترك الإحسان . وإنما لا يجوز ، لو كان الإحسان ممكناً في نفسه . أما إذا كان ممتنعاً في نفسه محالاً في ذاته ، لم يلزم منه النقص والإيجاد في الأزل : محال . لأنه عبارة عن الإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود ، وذلك لا ينقرر إلا فيها يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم . والأزل ينا في المسبوقية ، فكان الجمع بينهما محالاً . وهذا الكلام بعينه ، هو الجواب عن الحجة الثانية وأيضاً : فالنقص إنما يلزم [ أن ] لو لم يكن قادراً على تحصيل الملك والملكوت ، وهو تعالى في الأزل [ كان ] قادراً على تحصيله ، فلم يلزم من عدم الملك والملكوت نقصان . وأيضاً : فكل واحدة من هاتين الحجتين منقوض بحدوث الأشخاص المعينة ، وحدوث الحوادث اليومية وأجابوا عن السؤال الأول : بأن هذا [ الكلام ] إنما يتم إذا قلنا مسمّى الأزل مانع من الإيجاد والتكوين . وقد بينا بالدلائل القاطعة : أن ذلك باطل . على أنا نقول : إن هذا المانع كان زائلاً قبل أن خلق الله العالم بتقدير مائة سنة ، فكان الإلزام عائداً إليه وأما السؤال الثاني : فباطل أيضاً. لأن الأزل عندهم مانع من صحة الفعل ، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يكن الله متمكناً من تحصيل الملك والملكوت [ في الأزل ] ولا معنى للفقير الضعيف إلا ذلك . وهذا أيضاً يوجب العيب والضعف من وجه آخر ، وهو أن الامتناع من الإحسان في حق من كان قادراً على الإحسان ، ولا يضره ذلك الإحسان بوجه من الوجوه ، أقوى في العيب والنقصان منه في حق من لم يقدر عليه . فإذا قلتم : إله العالم كان قادراً على
For they say: [it follows] from the first proof abandoning the favor. And it is only not permissible if the favor was possible in itself. But if it is impossible in itself, absolutely impossible, then deficiency does not follow from it, and bringing into existence in eternity is impossible. Because it is an expression of bringing out from non-existence to existence, and that is only established in what is preceded by non-existence. And eternity precludes being preceded, so combining them is impossible. And this same statement is the answer to the second proof. And also: deficiency only follows if He were not capable of acquiring dominion and sovereignty, and He, exalted, in eternity was capable of acquiring it, so deficiency does not follow from the absence of dominion and sovereignty. And also: each of these two proofs is refuted by the occurrence of specific individuals and the occurrence of daily events. And they answered the first question: that this [statement] is only valid if we say that the meaning of eternity prevents bringing into existence and originating. And we have demonstrated with decisive proofs that that is false. Moreover, we say: that this preventer was removed before God created the world by an estimated hundred years, so the objection returns to it. As for the second question, it is also false. Because eternity, according to them, prevents the validity of action, and according to this estimation, God was not able to acquire dominion and sovereignty [in eternity], and there is no meaning for the poor and weak except that. And this also entails defect and weakness from another aspect: that refraining from favor on the part of one who is capable of favor and that favor does not harm him in any way is stronger in defect and deficiency than on the part of the one who is not capable of it. So when you say: the God of the world was capable of...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the first proof and the subsequent responses. The objector argues: the first proof would require God to abandon favor (tark al-iḥsān), but this is only wrong if the favor is possible. If eternal creation (al-ījād fī al-azal) is intrinsically impossible—because creation means bringing from non-existence to existence, which requires a temporal beginning—then eternity (al-azal) precludes that, so no deficiency arises from not doing the impossible. The objector then claims the same reasoning answers the second proof. Additionally, the objector says that deficiency only arises if God were incapable of acquiring dominion, but He always had the power, so no deficiency. Another objection: both proofs are contradicted by the temporal origination of specific individuals and daily events (i.e., creation happens in time, not eternally). The answer (from the author's side) responds to the first question: the objection stands only if 'eternity' is defined as preventing creation, but we have shown that is false. Moreover, even if that were a barrier, it ceased before creation (by about a hundred years), so the objection collapses. To the second question: if eternity prevented action, then God would not have been capable, but that would imply weakness—an even greater deficiency. Thus, the objection is turned back. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

الامتناع (al-imtināʿ)
الملكوت (al-malakūt)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الأزل (al-azal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal creation is impossible because creation requires a temporal beginning.
Correction: The impossibility is only assumed by defining eternity as a barrier; in reality, eternity does not prevent creation, as proven by decisive arguments.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that eternal creation is impossible?
A) By agreeing that eternity prevents creation.
B) By showing that the definition of eternity as preventing creation is false, and that any such barrier was removed before creation.
C) By denying that creation is an act of favor.
D) By claiming God does not need to create.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of eternal creation is not a valid objection; God's power is not limited by a false definition of eternity.

?
The author argues that defining eternity as preventing action leads to a greater deficiency in God. Have you ever described God's attributes in a way that actually diminishes His perfection? What is one attribute you need to re-examine so that you worship Him as He truly is?

The Third Argument for the Eternity of the World and Its Refutation
The author presents and refutes the argument that the world is eternal because its cause is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the eternity of the world and its refutation, which shows that an eternal cause does not necessarily produce an eternal effect because causation requires additional conditions.

Core Idea
An eternal cause does not automatically produce an eternal effect; causation requires conditions.

This lesson presents the third argument that the world must be eternal because its cause (God) is eternal. The author then anticipates a potential objection based on the meaning of generosity and provides a refutation, showing that causation involves more than just the existence of a cause—additional conditions are necessary, otherwise an infinite regress would follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three parts: the argument (first sentence), the objection (second sentence), and the refutation (rest).
The passage is dense with causal reasoning.
2
Identify the argument
The argument states: The cause of the world (God’s existence) is eternal, therefore the world (the effect) must be eternal. Identify the logical move being made.
It assumes that an eternal cause necessarily yields an eternal effect.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent says: If God’s generosity (jūd) is eternal, then His giving existence to the world is eternal, which is exactly what we claim. Recognize that this objection tries to collapse the distinction between cause and effect.
The term 'generosity' implies giving, which is an act in time.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author responds by saying that everything issuing from God must have an effecter, and the sum of considerations in effecting (the conditions) must be established. Otherwise, an infinite regress results. This shows that additional factors are needed for the effect to occur, so the world is not automatically eternal.
The key is that causation is not just the existence of a cause, but also involves conditions (like divine will).
5
Reflect on the key insight
Ask yourself: How does this argument fail? Why is an eternal cause not sufficient for an eternal effect? The answer lies in the need for additional conditions, which the argument overlooks.
This refutation is a classic move in Islamic kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : علة وجود العالم : وجود الباري، تعالى ، ووجوده أزلي . فعلة وجود العالم أزلية فيلزم كون العالم أزلياً . فإن قالوا : الجود هو الإفادة والتحصيل . فقولكم : جود البارى تعالى : أزلي . معناه : أن كونه موجداً للعالم أزلي ، وذلك عين المطلوب ، قلنا : تحقيق هذا الكلام : إن جملة ما يصدر عن البارى تعالى لا بد له من مؤثر فمجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المصدرية : قائم وإلا لزم افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر ولزم التسلسل
The third argument: The cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator, exalted, and His existence is eternal. So the cause of the world's existence is eternal, therefore the world must be eternal. If they say: Generosity is giving and obtaining. So your statement: the generosity of the Creator, exalted, is eternal, means that His being the originator of the world is eternal, and that is exactly what is sought. We say: The verification of this statement is that the totality of what issues from the Creator, exalted, must have an effecter; so the sum of the considerations in the effecting is established, otherwise it would necessitate its need for another effecter and lead to infinite regress.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the eternity of the world: the world's cause (God's existence) is eternal, so the effect (the world) must also be eternal. It then anticipates an objection that the term 'generosity' (al-jūd) implies giving, so eternal generosity means eternal giving, which is the same as the claim. The author responds by stating that the verification of this claim is that everything issuing from God must have an effecter; the sum of the considerations in the effecting (i.e., the conditions for causation) must be established, otherwise it would lead to an infinite regress. This implies that the effect is not automatically eternal just because the cause is eternal, because the causation depends on additional factors (such as divine will).

Key Terms
العلة
العالم
الجود
المصدرية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the cause of something is eternal, the effect must also be eternal.
Correction: An eternal cause does not necessarily produce an eternal effect because causation depends on additional conditions, such as divine will and the moment of creation.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the third argument's claim that the world must be eternal because its cause is eternal?
A) He agrees with it.
B) He refutes it by showing that causation depends on additional conditions, otherwise infinite regress follows.
C) He says it is irrelevant because the world is not an effect.
D) He argues that the cause is not truly eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world is not necessarily eternal just because its cause (God) is eternal; causation requires conditions, otherwise infinite regress would follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Proof: Eternity of the World from Eternal Cause
Al-Razi presents a proof for the world's eternity from the eternity of its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternity of God as cause necessitates the eternity of the world according to kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
An eternal cause necessarily produces an eternal effect.

In this passage, the author presents a third argument for the world's eternity based on causality. He argues that since God's existence is eternal and He is the cause of the world, the world must also be eternal. He then addresses a potential objection about the meaning of generosity. This lesson unpacks the logic and shows why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: proof, objection, response.
The Arabic is concise; the translation clarifies the logical steps.
2
Identify the core premise
The proof begins: the cause of the world's existence is God's existence, which is eternal. Therefore the cause is eternal, so the world (effect) must be eternal. Spot this cause-effect reasoning.
3
Analyze the objection
The opponent argues that calling God's generosity eternal is just another way of saying He eternally creates the world, which is the very point in dispute. Understand this as a charge of circularity.
The objection targets the definition of 'generosity' (al-jūd).
4
Follow the rebuttal
The author responds by verifying the underlying condition: everything emanating from God requires an efficient cause, and the set of conditions for being a source must be eternal to avoid infinite regress. Hence the effects are eternal.
This reframes the argument in terms of necessary conditions.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author notes that this proof is essentially the same as the first proof. Consider how this argument strengthens the case for the world's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : علة وجود العالم : وجود الباري، تعالى ، ووجوده أزلي . فعلة وجود العالم أزلية فيلزم كون العالم أزلياً . فإن قالوا : الجود هو الإفادة والتحصيل . فقولكم : جود البارى تعالى : أزلي . معناه : أن كونه موجداً للعالم أزلي ، وذلك عين المطلوب ، قلنا : تحقيق هذا الكلام : إن جملة ما يصدر عن البارى تعالى لا بد له من مؤثر فمجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المصدرية : قائم وإلا لزم افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر ولزم التسلسل ، وإذا كان مجموع تلك الأمور أزلياً ، يلزم أن تكون الآثار الصادرة عن الباريء أزلية ، وعلى هذا التقدير ، فهذا الكلام هو عين الحجة الأولى
The third proof: The cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator, exalted, and His existence is eternal. So the cause of the existence of the world is eternal, so it follows that the world is eternal. If they say: Generosity is giving and bestowal. So your statement: the generosity of the Creator, exalted, is eternal, means that He is the Creator of the world eternally, and that is exactly the desired conclusion. We say: The verification of this statement is that the totality of what emanates from the Creator, exalted, must have an efficient cause. So the sum of the considerations in [the act of] being a source is established; otherwise it would entail needing another efficient cause and entail infinite regress. And when that sum of considerations is eternal, it follows that the effects emanating from the Creator are eternal. And on this estimation, this statement is exactly the first proof.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) for the eternity of the world. The argument begins: the cause (ʿilla) of the world's existence is the existence of God, and God's existence is eternal; therefore the cause is eternal, so the effect (the world) must be eternal. The author then anticipates an objection: the opponent might say that 'generosity' (al-jūd) means giving and bestowing, so saying 'God's generosity is eternal' means that His act of creating the world is eternal, which is exactly the claim under dispute. The author responds by verifying the reasoning: everything that emanates from God requires an efficient cause (muʾaththir); the total set of conditions for being a source (al-masdariyya) must be present, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. Since that set of conditions is eternal, the effects (the world) are eternal. He concludes that this is essentially the same as the first proof.

Key Terms
علة وجود العالم (cause of the world's existence)
الجود (generosity)
المؤثر (efficient cause)
المصدرية (being a source)
التسلسل (infinite regress)
الآثار (effects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes the world is eternal to prove it eternal.
Correction: The author clarifies that the argument is based on the eternal cause, not a circular assumption; the objection misinterprets 'generosity' as merely the act of creation, while the proof relies on the necessary conditions for emanation being eternal.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what does the author argue about the cause of the world's existence?
A) It is temporal
B) It is eternal
C) It is contingent
D) It is unknown

Key Takeaway
The third proof demonstrates that the eternity of God as the cause logically entails the eternity of the world.

?
The argument shows that infinite regress is impossible. Consider the causes of your own faith and actions: do they trace back to an eternal foundation, or are they caught in a chain of temporary motivations? What one step can you take today to root your actions more firmly in the eternal?

The Three Possibilities for Divine Motive
The author enumerates three possible divine motives and invalidates two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's motive for creation must be for benefit, based on the three possibilities presented.

Core Idea
God's motive for creation cannot be harm or futility, only benefit.

In this alternative formulation of the third proof, the author considers three possible motives for God's act of creation. By eliminating the impossible options, we arrive at the conclusion that creation is an act of benevolence. This lesson walks through that tripartite division and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
The passage uses a question-and-elimination structure.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find the three options for divine motive: benefit, harm, or neither. Notice how each is introduced.
3
Understand why harming is impossible
Reflect on why a harmful motive contradicts God's mercy. The text says it is 'not befitting' the Benevolent and Merciful.
This is a theological objection, not a logical contradiction.
4
Understand why futility is impossible
Consider why acting without any purpose (futility) is absurd for a wise being. The text labels it as 'futile' (عبث).
This relies on the principle that God's actions have wisdom.
5
Conclude that motive is benefit
With two options eliminated, the only remaining possibility is benefit. Note how the author states this as established.
This sets up the next part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن تقريره من وجه آخر : فيقال : إنه تعالى أراد إيجاد العالم ، لداعية الانتفاع ، أو لداعية الإضرار ، أو لا لواحدة من هاتين الداعيتين ؟ والثاني محال ، لأن الإيجاد لداعية الإضرار ، لا يليق بالمحسن الرحيم . والثالث : وهو أن الإيجاد لا لداعية الانتفاع ولا لداعية الإضرار عبث ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت أنه تعالى خلق الخلق لداعية الإنتفاع . ثم نقول : هذه الداعية ، إما أن تكون حادثة ، أو قديمة . فإن كانت حادثة عاد التقسيم المذكور في حدوثها ، وإن كانت قديمة. فإما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل مانع يمنع من صدور هذا الأثر ، أو لم يحصل . والأول باطل ، لأنه لو حصل لما زال المانع لما ثبت أن الأزلي لا يزول . فيثبت : أن الداعي إلى الإحسان ، كان حاصلا في الأزل ، وأن المانع منه ، كان مفقوداً . فهذا هو المراد من قول القدماء : إن علة وجود العالم هي وجود البارىء ، ووجوده أزلي ، فيكون وجود العالم : أزلياً
And it can be presented from another aspect: it is said: He, exalted, intended to bring the world into existence either for a motive of benefiting, or for a motive of harming, or for neither of these two motives? The second is impossible, because bringing into existence for a motive of harming is not befitting the Benevolent, the Merciful. And the third, which is bringing into existence not for a motive of benefiting nor for a motive of harming, is futile. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it is established that He, exalted, created creation for a motive of benefiting. Then we say: this motive is either temporal or eternal. If it is temporal, the aforementioned division applies to its coming-to-be. And if it is eternal, then either it is said that in pre-eternity there was an obstacle preventing the emanation of this effect, or there was not. The first is invalid, because if it existed, it would not cease, since it is established that the eternal does not cease. So it is established that the motive for benevolence existed in pre-eternity and that the obstacle was absent. This is the meaning of the saying of the ancients: the cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator, and His existence is eternal, so the existence of the world is eternal.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an alternative formulation of the third proof, focusing on the divine motive (dāʿiya). The author considers three possibilities for God's motive in creating: (1) for benefit, (2) for harm, or (3) for neither. The second (harm) is impossible because it contradicts God's mercy; the third (futility) is also absurd. Thus the motive must be for benefit. Next, the author examines whether this motive is temporal (ḥāditha) or eternal (qadīma). If temporal, then the same division applies to its origin, leading to an infinite regress or a first eternal motive. If eternal, then either there was an obstacle in pre-eternity preventing creation, or there was not. If there was an obstacle, it would never cease because eternal things do not cease, so creation would never happen. Since creation did happen, there was no obstacle. Therefore the motive for benevolence existed eternally and the obstacle was absent. This is exactly what the ancients meant: the cause of the world is God's existence, and since His existence is eternal, the world is eternal.

Key Terms
الداعية (motive)
الانتفاع (benefiting)
الإضرار (harming)
العبث (futility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the possibility of a harmful motive impossible?
A) Because God is merciful
B) Because harm is futile
C) Because it would be an obstacle
D) Because it is temporal

Key Takeaway
God's motive for creation must be for benefit, as harm and futility are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God only creates for benefit, then what about the trials and hardships in your life? Are you able to see them as part of God's ultimate benefit, or do you sometimes doubt His wisdom when you suffer? How can you train yourself to trust that even your worst moments are for your good?
?
The passage eliminates harm as a motive for God. Have you ever attributed a painful event to God's cruelty or indifference? How does this truth challenge that assumption and change the way you view your past difficulties?
?
If creation is not futile, then every moment of your existence has a purpose. Are you living as if your life is a purposeful creation of a wise God, or do you sometimes act as if it's all random? What would you do differently today if you fully believed that nothing God does is pointless?

Eternal Motive and the Absence of Obstacle
The author examines whether the divine motive is temporal or eternal and argues for eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from eternal motive and absence of obstacle leads to the conclusion that the world is eternal.

Core Idea
An eternal motive with no obstacle means the world must be eternal.

Having established that God's motive for creation is benefit, the author now examines whether that motive is temporal or eternal. This leads to a crucial argument about the presence or absence of an obstacle in pre-eternity, culminating in the conclusion that the world is eternal, as the ancients held.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the logical structure: first split on temporal/eternal, then obstacle/no obstacle.
This passage builds on the conclusion from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the two possibilities for the motive
The motive is either temporal (ḥāditha) or eternal (qadīma). If temporal, the same division applies to its origin, leading to infinite regress.
The text says 'the aforementioned division applies' - this refers to the earlier elimination.
3
Consider the eternal case with or without obstacle
If the motive is eternal, there was either an obstacle in pre-eternity preventing the effect, or there was not.
4
Why an eternal obstacle is impossible
If an obstacle existed eternally, it would never cease (since eternal things do not cease). Thus creation would never happen. Since it did, there was no obstacle.
This uses the principle that the eternal is unchangeable.
5
Conclude that the world is eternal
With the motive eternally present and no obstacle, the world's existence follows eternally from God's existence. This is the meaning of the ancients' saying.
The phrase 'cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator' links back to the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن تقريره من وجه آخر : فيقال : إنه تعالى أراد إيجاد العالم ، لداعية الانتفاع ، أو لداعية الإضرار ، أو لا لواحدة من هاتين الداعيتين ؟ والثاني محال ، لأن الإيجاد لداعية الإضرار ، لا يليق بالمحسن الرحيم . والثالث : وهو أن الإيجاد لا لداعية الانتفاع ولا لداعية الإضرار عبث ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت أنه تعالى خلق الخلق لداعية الإنتفاع . ثم نقول : هذه الداعية ، إما أن تكون حادثة ، أو قديمة . فإن كانت حادثة عاد التقسيم المذكور في حدوثها ، وإن كانت قديمة. فإما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل مانع يمنع من صدور هذا الأثر ، أو لم يحصل . والأول باطل ، لأنه لو حصل لما زال المانع لما ثبت أن الأزلي لا يزول . فيثبت : أن الداعي إلى الإحسان ، كان حاصلا في الأزل ، وأن المانع منه ، كان مفقوداً . فهذا هو المراد من قول القدماء : إن علة وجود العالم هي وجود البارىء ، ووجوده أزلي ، فيكون وجود العالم : أزلياً
And it can be presented from another aspect: it is said: He, exalted, intended to bring the world into existence either for a motive of benefiting, or for a motive of harming, or for neither of these two motives? The second is impossible, because bringing into existence for a motive of harming is not befitting the Benevolent, the Merciful. And the third, which is bringing into existence not for a motive of benefiting nor for a motive of harming, is futile. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it is established that He, exalted, created creation for a motive of benefiting. Then we say: this motive is either temporal or eternal. If it is temporal, the aforementioned division applies to its coming-to-be. And if it is eternal, then either it is said that in pre-eternity there was an obstacle preventing the emanation of this effect, or there was not. The first is invalid, because if it existed, it would not cease, since it is established that the eternal does not cease. So it is established that the motive for benevolence existed in pre-eternity and that the obstacle was absent. This is the meaning of the saying of the ancients: the cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator, and His existence is eternal, so the existence of the world is eternal.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an alternative formulation of the third proof, focusing on the divine motive (dāʿiya). The author considers three possibilities for God's motive in creating: (1) for benefit, (2) for harm, or (3) for neither. The second (harm) is impossible because it contradicts God's mercy; the third (futility) is also absurd. Thus the motive must be for benefit. Next, the author examines whether this motive is temporal (ḥāditha) or eternal (qadīma). If temporal, then the same division applies to its origin, leading to an infinite regress or a first eternal motive. If eternal, then either there was an obstacle in pre-eternity preventing creation, or there was not. If there was an obstacle, it would never cease because eternal things do not cease, so creation would never happen. Since creation did happen, there was no obstacle. Therefore the motive for benevolence existed eternally and the obstacle was absent. This is exactly what the ancients meant: the cause of the world is God's existence, and since His existence is eternal, the world is eternal.

Key Terms
الحادثة (temporal)
القديمة (eternal)
المَانِع (obstacle)
الأزلي (the eternal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's motive could be temporal, leading to creation in time.
Correction: The argument shows that a temporal motive would lead to infinite regress, so the motive must be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the possibility of an eternal obstacle invalid?
A) Because the obstacle would be temporal
B) Because the obstacle would cease
C) Because the eternal does not cease
D) Because the motive is temporal

Key Takeaway
The divine motive for benefit is eternal and unhindered, implying the world is eternal as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing can hinder God's will, even in your own life? When you face delays in your duas, do you doubt that His motive for goodness is eternal and unhindered? What would it take for you to trust that His motive is always present and unobstructed?
?
If the world is co-eternal with God, does that diminish God's sovereignty in your heart, or does it magnify His perfection? Examine your own belief: have you ever thought that God's creation was a reaction to something, rather than an eternal outpouring of His generosity? How does this argument challenge your assumption that God 'decided' to create at a certain time?
?
Consider the concept that God's motive is eternal. Does your daily worship reflect a God who is always benevolent, or do you sometimes act as if He needs to be 'moved' by your actions? How would your relationship with Allah change if you fully internalized that His goodness toward you has no beginning and no obstacle?

An Alternative Formulation of the Eternity Argument
Alternative formulation of the first argument using God's motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the alternative formulation of the first argument for the eternity of the world proceeds from analyzing God's motive for creation.

Core Idea
God's motive for creation must be eternal and without obstacle, so the world is eternal.

This passage presents a different way of stating the first argument for the eternity of the world. Instead of focusing on God's attributes, it examines the motive behind creation. By analyzing whether God's motive is for benefit or harm, temporal or eternal, and whether any obstacle existed, it arrives at the conclusion that the world must be eternal. Let's follow this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the logical flow. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice how the argument moves from motive to obstacle.
2
Analyze the motive division
The text divides God's possible motives into three: for benefit, for harm, or for no motive. Identify why the latter two are rejected.
This step establishes that creation is for benefit.
3
Examine temporal vs eternal motive
The motive itself must be either temporal or eternal. Understand the implications of each: temporal leads to infinite regress; eternal leads to obstacle consideration.
The obstacle is key: an eternal obstacle cannot cease.
4
Understand the final conclusion
With motive eternal and no obstacle, the world's existence must be eternal, as the ancients said.
This argument relies on the principle that the existence of the Creator is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجموع تلك الأمور أزلياً ، يلزم أن تكون الآثار الصادرة عن الباريء أزلية ، وعلى هذا التقدير ، فهذا الكلام هو عين الحجة الأولى · ويمكن تقريره من وجه آخر : فيقال : إنه تعالى أراد إيجاد العالم ، لداعية الانتفاع ، أو لداعية الإضرار ، أو لا لواحدة من هاتين الداعيتين ؟ والثاني محال ، لأن الإيجاد لداعية الإضرار ، لا يليق بالمحسن الرحيم . والثالث : وهو [أن] الإيجاد لا لداعية الانتفاع ولا لداعية الإضرار عبث ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت أنه تعالى خلق الخلق لداعية الإنتفاع . ثم نقول : هذه الداعية ، إما أن تكون حادثة ، أو قديمة . فإن كانت حادثة عاد التقسيم المذكور في حدوثها ، وإن كانت قديمة. فإما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل مانع يمنع من صدور هذا الأثر ، أو لم يحصل . والأول باطل ، لأنه لو حصل لما زال المانع لما ثبت أن الأزلي لا يزول . فيثبت : أن الداعي إلى الإحسان ، كان حاصلا في الأزل ، وأن المانع منه ، كان مفقوداً . فهذا هو المراد من قول القدماء : إن علة وجود العالم هي وجود البارىء ، ووجوده أزلي ، فيكون وجود العالم : أزلياً
The sum of those matters being eternal, it is necessary that the effects emanating from the Creator be eternal. And upon this estimation, this statement is exactly the first argument. It can be formulated in another way: It is said: He, exalted, intended to bring the world into existence, either for a motive of benefit, or for a motive of harm, or for neither of these two motives. The second is impossible, because bringing into existence for a motive of harm is not befitting the Beneficent, the Merciful. The third—which is that bringing into existence is neither for a motive of benefit nor for a motive of harm—is folly. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it is established that He, exalted, created creation for a motive of benefit. Then we say: This motive is either temporal or eternal. If it is temporal, the aforementioned division applies to its temporality. And if it is eternal, then it is said: Either there occurred in eternity an obstacle preventing the emanation of this effect, or there did not. The first is false, for if it had occurred, it would never cease, because it is established that the eternal does not cease. So it is established that the motive for beneficence was present in eternity and that the obstacle to it was absent. This is the meaning of the saying of the ancients: The cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator, and His existence is eternal, so the existence of the world is eternal.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage reformulates the first argument for the eternity of the world. It begins by stating that the previous reasoning (sum of matters being eternal) leads to the conclusion that the world's effects are eternal. This alternative presentation starts with God's motive for creation. It posits three possibilities: God intended to create for a motive of benefit, for a motive of harm, or for no motive. The second is impossible because it contradicts God's mercy. The third is impossible because it would be pointless. Therefore creation must have been for a motive of benefit. Next, it considers whether this motive is temporal or eternal. If temporal, the same division applies to its own origin, leading to an infinite regress. If eternal, then either there was an eternal obstacle preventing creation or not. An eternal obstacle is impossible because it would never cease, so the motive was present and no obstacle existed. Hence, the world's existence is as eternal as the Creator's existence, which is the ancients' view.

Key Terms
داعية (motive)
حادثة (temporal)
قديمة (eternal)
مانع (obstacle)
إحسان (beneficence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument implies God is needy or compelled by a motive.
Correction: The argument does not claim God is needy; it only examines the logical implications of attributing a temporal motive to God, concluding that an eternal motive is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence if God's motive for creation is temporal?

B) The same division about its origin applies, leading to infinite regress.
C) It shows God is imperfect.
D) It is impossible because God's motive must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world is eternal because God's motive for creation is eternal and unobstructed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you living as if you are a gift of divine generosity, or do you sometimes feel like a cosmic accident? How would your daily actions change if you truly believed you were created out of sheer goodness?
?
The argument states that God's motive for your existence is eternal—He always intended to create you. Yet, you might struggle with feelings of insignificance or doubt your place in the universe. How does knowing that your existence was eternally desired by the Creator confront those feelings? What would it mean to fully accept that you are not an afterthought but an eternal intention?
?
Since there was never any obstacle in God's eternal plan for you, what obstacles do you create in your own life that prevent you from fulfilling the purpose for which you were eternally intended? Can you identify one specific barrier—fear, pride, neglect—that you need to remove today to align with His motive for your creation?

Refuting Benefit to the Creator as Reason for Specific Creation Time
Refutes benefit to Creator as reason for specific creation time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific time of creation cannot be explained by a benefit returning to the Creator, through four logical arguments.

Core Idea
God's perfection cannot be motivated by need or deficiency.

This passage tackles a key objection to the doctrine of temporal creation: if God created the world at a specific moment, that moment must have been chosen for a reason. The theologian here examines the possibility that the reason was a benefit to God Himself, and shows why this is impossible. You will see four concise arguments that highlight the incompatibility of need with divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source text carefully, noting how the argument is structured: it first sets up a dichotomy, then eliminates one side with four reasons.
The passage begins with 'الحجة الرابعة' and continues through the four reasons.
2
Identify the four reasons
Locate the four numbered reasons (الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع) that refute the idea of benefit returning to the Creator.
Each reason is a separate logical point.
3
Understand the logical flow
Notice how the argument proceeds: first, it dismisses creation without wisdom; then it splits the remaining possibility into benefit to Creator or creature; then it refutes benefit to Creator with four sub-arguments.
The key is that any benefit to God would imply deficiency, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important for Islamic theology: it protects God's absolute independence and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : أن نقول : لو قلنا : إنه تعالى أحدث العالم في وقت معين، لكان تخصيص الإحداث بذلك الوقت، إما أن يكون الحكمة ومصلحة ، أو لا لحكمة ومصلحة ، والأول باطل . لأن تلك المصلحة ، إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الخالق ، أو إلى المخلوق والأول : محال . لوجوه : الأول : أنه يلزم أن يقال : إنه تعالى في محل النفع والضرر . والثاني : أن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، فتلك الحاجة ، إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة ، فإن كانت قديمة، لزم من قدمها، قدم الفعل . وإن كانت حادثة ، عاد الطلب في أنه : لم حدثت تلك الحاجة في ذلك الوقت ، دون ما قبله ، وما بعده ؟ . والثالث : أن على هذا التقدير يكون إله العالم ناقصاً محتاجاً إلى الاستكمال بالغير . الرابع : أن إله العالم قديم أزلي واجب لذاته . فكان أكمل حالا من المحدثات ، وكون الأكمل مستكملاً بالأخس الأدون : محال . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أحدث العالم لنفع عائد إلى الغير، فهذا أيضاً باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الإيجاد لو كان إحساناً إلى المخلوق ، لكان إما أن يكون إحساناً إليه حال عدمه ، أو حال وجوده . والأول باطل . لأنه ما دام يكون معدوماً ، فالوجود غير واصل إليه ، فامتنع كون الإيجاد إحساناً إلى المعدوم . والثاني : أيضاً باطل ، لأن إيصال الوجود إليه بعد وجوده يقتضي إيجاد الموجود وأنه محال. لا يقال : الإيجاد إنما يكون إحساناً من حيث إنه بعد دخوله في الوجود ، يستعد لأن يصل إليه أنواع اللذة والسرور . لأنـا نقول : الكلام إنما وقع في أن الإيجاد ليس بإحسان ، وما ذكرتموه لا يدفع ذلك . فإنكم سلمتم أن نفس الإيجاد ليس إحساناً . وإنما الإحسان ما يحصل بعد ذلك ، ونحن ما ادعينا حالاً أن ابتداء الخلق ليس بإحسان
The fourth argument: We say: If we say that He, exalted, originated the world at a specific time, then the specification of origination to that time would either be due to wisdom and benefit or not due to wisdom and benefit. The first is false. Because that benefit is either returning to the Creator or to the creature. The first is impossible, for reasons: First: It would necessitate that it be said that He, exalted, is a locus of benefit and harm. Second: Assuming that it is so, that need is either eternal or temporal. If it is eternal, then from its eternity follows the eternity of the act. If it is temporal, then the inquiry returns as to why that need occurred at that time and not before or after. Third: Upon this estimation, the God of the world would be deficient, in need of completion by another. Fourth: The God of the world is eternal, pre-eternal, necessary in essence. He is the most perfect in state compared to originated things, and for the most perfect to be completed by the basest and lowliest is impossible. As for the second division—that it is said that He, exalted, originated the world only for a benefit returning to another—this too is false. Proofs for it: First: If bringing into existence were beneficence to the creature, it would either be beneficence to him in the state of his non-existence or in the state of his existence. The first is false because as long as he is non-existent, existence does not reach him, so it is impossible for bringing into existence to be beneficence to the non-existent. The second is also false, because conveying existence to him after his existence would require bringing the existent into existence, which is impossible. It cannot be said: Bringing into existence is beneficence in that after his entry into existence, he becomes disposed for various kinds of pleasure and joy to reach him. For we say: The discussion is only about whether bringing into existence is not beneficence, and what you mentioned does not refute that. For you have conceded that the very bringing into existence is not beneficence; rather, beneficence is what occurs after that. And we have not claimed that the beginning of creation is not beneficence.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the view that the world was created at a specific time. It assumes the attribution of a specific time for creation and examines the possible reasons: either there is a wisdom/benefit in that specific time or not. If not, it is arbitrary. If there is a benefit, it returns either to the Creator or to the creature. The first is refuted by four reasons: (1) It would imply God is subject to benefit and harm. (2) If the need is eternal, the act must be eternal; if temporal, the same question applies to its origin. (3) It would imply God is deficient and needs completion. (4) God is perfect, so being completed by what is inferior is impossible. The second possibility—benefit to the creature—is also false. Two proofs are given: (1) Beneficence cannot apply to the non-existent, as existence does not reach them; (2) After existence, conveying existence again is impossible. A potential objection that beneficence lies in the subsequent pleasures is refuted: the act of creation itself is not beneficence; beneficence is what follows. The argument is cut off but the main point is clear.

Key Terms
مصلحة (benefit/wisdom)
حاجة (need)
ناقص (deficient)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is one of the four reasons given for why benefit returning to the Creator cannot explain the specific time of creation?
A) It would make God the locus of benefit and harm.
B) It would prove the world is eternal.
C) It would show that God is merciful.
D) It would imply creation is pointless.

Key Takeaway
The specific time of creation cannot be due to a benefit returning to God, as that would imply deficiency in His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first reason states that saying God benefits from creation would make Him a locus of benefit and harm. Have you ever, even subtly, thought that your obedience benefits God or that your sin harms Him? How does this first reason correct that assumption in your heart right now?
?
The second reason presents a dilemma about the need being eternal or temporal. Think about a time you felt God 'needed' to do something for you at a specific moment. According to this argument, what does that imply about your understanding of God's perfection? What change must you make in your theological worldview?
?
The third and fourth reasons argue that God would be deficient if He needed creation. As you go about your daily life, do you subconsciously act as if God depends on your actions to maintain His glory? How can you internalize that God is al-ghaniyy (the Self-Sufficient) so that your worship becomes purely about your need for Him, not His need for you?
