Refuting Benefit to Creatures as Reason for Specific Creation Time
Refutes benefit to creatures as reason for specific creation time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific time of creation cannot be explained by a benefit returning to creatures, and why the act of creation itself is not beneficence.

Core Idea
Beneficence cannot apply to the non-existent or to the already existent.

After dismissing benefit to the Creator, the theologian turns to the possibility that creation at a specific time was for the benefit of the creatures. This passage argues that such a view is also false. The key insight is that the act of bringing into existence cannot itself be beneficence, because beneficence requires a recipient who experiences good. You will see two proofs and a refutation of a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the argument
Read from 'وأما القسم الثاني' through the end of the provided text. Notice how it first states the claim, then gives two proofs, then handles an objection.
This is a complete argument starting with 'وأما القسم الثاني'.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate the two proofs against benefit to the creature: (1) beneficence to the non-existent is impossible, (2) beneficence to the existent would require re-creating the already existent.
These are labeled as 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Understand the objection: some might say that creation is beneficence because it enables future pleasures. The response clarifies that the act of creation itself is not beneficence; the beneficence comes afterward.
The objection begins with 'لا يقال' and the response with 'لأنا نقول'.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this fits into the larger fourth argument: both possibilities (benefit to Creator and benefit to creature) are eliminated, leaving only the conclusion that the specific time is not due to any benefit.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : أن نقول : لو قلنا : إنه تعالى أحدث العالم في وقت معين، لكان تخصيص الإحداث بذلك الوقت، إما أن يكون الحكمة ومصلحة ، أو لا لحكمة ومصلحة ، والأول باطل . لأن تلك المصلحة ، إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الخالق ، أو إلى المخلوق والأول : محال . لوجوه : الأول : أنه يلزم أن يقال : إنه تعالى في محل النفع والضرر . والثاني : أن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، فتلك الحاجة ، إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة ، فإن كانت قديمة، لزم من قدمها، قدم الفعل . وإن كانت حادثة ، عاد الطلب في أنه : لم حدثت تلك الحاجة في ذلك الوقت ، دون ما قبله ، وما بعده ؟ . والثالث : أن على هذا التقدير يكون إله العالم ناقصاً محتاجاً إلى الاستكمال بالغير . الرابع : أن إله العالم قديم أزلي واجب لذاته . فكان أكمل حالا من المحدثات ، وكون الأكمل مستكملاً بالأخس الأدون : محال . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أحدث العالم لنفع عائد إلى الغير، فهذا أيضاً باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الإيجاد لو كان إحساناً إلى المخلوق ، لكان إما أن يكون إحساناً إليه حال عدمه ، أو حال وجوده . والأول باطل . لأنه ما دام يكون معدوماً ، فالوجود غير واصل إليه ، فامتنع كون الإيجاد إحساناً إلى المعدوم . والثاني : أيضاً باطل ، لأن إيصال الوجود إليه بعد وجوده يقتضي إيجاد الموجود وأنه محال. لا يقال : الإيجاد إنما يكون إحساناً من حيث إنه بعد دخوله في الوجود ، يستعد لأن يصل إليه أنواع اللذة والسرور . لأنـا نقول : الكلام إنما وقع في أن الإيجاد ليس بإحسان ، وما ذكرتموه لا يدفع ذلك . فإنكم سلمتم أن نفس الإيجاد ليس إحساناً . وإنما الإحسان ما يحصل بعد ذلك ، ونحن ما ادعينا حالاً أن ابتداء الخلق ليس بإحسان
The fourth argument: We say: If we say that He, exalted, originated the world at a specific time, then the specification of origination to that time would either be due to wisdom and benefit or not due to wisdom and benefit. The first is false. Because that benefit is either returning to the Creator or to the creature. The first is impossible, for reasons: First: It would necessitate that it be said that He, exalted, is a locus of benefit and harm. Second: Assuming that it is so, that need is either eternal or temporal. If it is eternal, then from its eternity follows the eternity of the act. If it is temporal, then the inquiry returns as to why that need occurred at that time and not before or after. Third: Upon this estimation, the God of the world would be deficient, in need of completion by another. Fourth: The God of the world is eternal, pre-eternal, necessary in essence. He is the most perfect in state compared to originated things, and for the most perfect to be completed by the basest and lowliest is impossible. As for the second division—that it is said that He, exalted, originated the world only for a benefit returning to another—this too is false. Proofs for it: First: If bringing into existence were beneficence to the creature, it would either be beneficence to him in the state of his non-existence or in the state of his existence. The first is false because as long as he is non-existent, existence does not reach him, so it is impossible for bringing into existence to be beneficence to the non-existent. The second is also false, because conveying existence to him after his existence would require bringing the existent into existence, which is impossible. It cannot be said: Bringing into existence is beneficence in that after his entry into existence, he becomes disposed for various kinds of pleasure and joy to reach him. For we say: The discussion is only about whether bringing into existence is not beneficence, and what you mentioned does not refute that. For you have conceded that the very bringing into existence is not beneficence; rather, beneficence is what occurs after that. And we have not claimed that the beginning of creation is not beneficence.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the view that the world was created at a specific time. It assumes the attribution of a specific time for creation and examines the possible reasons: either there is a wisdom/benefit in that specific time or not. If not, it is arbitrary. If there is a benefit, it returns either to the Creator or to the creature. The first is refuted by four reasons: (1) It would imply God is subject to benefit and harm. (2) If the need is eternal, the act must be eternal; if temporal, the same question applies to its origin. (3) It would imply God is deficient and needs completion. (4) God is perfect, so being completed by what is inferior is impossible. The second possibility—benefit to the creature—is also false. Two proofs are given: (1) Beneficence cannot apply to the non-existent, as existence does not reach them; (2) After existence, conveying existence again is impossible. A potential objection that beneficence lies in the subsequent pleasures is refuted: the act of creation itself is not beneficence; beneficence is what follows. The argument is cut off but the main point is clear.

Key Terms
إحسان (beneficence)
إيجاد (bringing into existence)
معدوم (non-existent)
موجود (existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation at a specific time is for the benefit of the creatures.
Correction: The act of creation itself cannot be beneficence because the creature does not yet exist to receive it, and if already existent, recreating is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for creation to be beneficence to the creature?
A) Because the creature does not exist yet when creation occurs.
B) Because God does not care about creatures.
C) Because only the Creator benefits from creation.
D) Because creatures are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The act of creation cannot be for the benefit of creatures because beneficence requires pre-existent recipients.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not create for your benefit, why should you feel entitled to His blessings? Examine your heart: Do you often feel that God owes you something? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
When you pray for something, do you ever think that God should grant it because it benefits you? How does the fact that God's actions are not motivated by your benefit affect your relationship with Him and your humility in supplication?
?
This lesson states that creation cannot be a benefit to the non-existent. But you are existent now, and every moment you exist is a gift not owed. Are you truly grateful for the very fact of your existence, or do you only appreciate benefits that come after you already exist? What can you do today to cultivate deeper gratitude for the sheer gift of being?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
The Sixth Argument: Suffering in This World Refutes Beneficence
Al-Razi opens the sixth argument by contrasting assumption with empirical reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses empirical observation of widespread suffering to challenge the claim that creation is an act of beneficence.

Core Idea
The world is filled with suffering, not comfort — so beneficence is contradicted.

The previous five arguments in this debate assumed that humans have all they desire. But al-Razi now turns to the reality we see around us: a world filled with grief, fear, and calamity. This empirical observation forms the core of the sixth argument, which directly challenges the notion that creation is an act of beneficence. Although the passage cuts off mid-sentence, the direction is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with its context
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that this is the beginning of the sixth argument, which breaks from previous purely rational arguments by appealing to observable reality.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off after mentioning the Hereafter.
2
Identify the key contrast
The passage contrasts a hypothetical scenario where everything desired is present with the actual state of the world, which is the opposite. Find the phrase that signals this shift.
The key phrase is 'وليس الأمر كذلك' (but the matter is not so).
3
Examine the list of sufferings
The passage lists many types of suffering: griefs, worries, sorrows, fears, calamities, burning, drowning, decrepitude, blindness, chronic illness. Reflect on how this empirical evidence challenges the idea of beneficence.
This list is not exhaustive but illustrates the prevalence of pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس: أن نقول: إن الوجوه الخمسة السالفة إنما نذكرها لو قدرنا أن كل ما اشتهاه الإنسان وأراده ومال طبعه إليه، يكون حاضراً. وليس الأمر كذلك، لأنا نرى أن أحوال أهل الدنيا بالعكس من ذلك. فإنا نرى أن أكثرهم يكونون في الغموم والهموم والأحزان والمخاوف والمتالف والحرق والغرق والهرم والعمى والزمانة. وأما في الآخرة
The sixth aspect: that we say: the five preceding aspects are only mentioned if we were to suppose that everything that a person desires, wants, and his nature inclines to, is present. But the matter is not so, because we see that the conditions of the people of this world are the opposite of that. For we see that most of them are in griefs, worries, sorrows, fears, calamities, burning, drowning, decrepitude, blindness, and chronic illness. And as for the Hereafter
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage opens the sixth argument, which is empirical rather than purely rational. The author notes that the previous five arguments assumed that everything humans desire is available, but reality shows the opposite. He points to the widespread suffering in this world: grief, worry, sorrow, fear, calamities, fire, drowning, old age, blindness, and chronic illness. The text cuts off at 'And as for the Hereafter', presumably to contrast the afterlife. Although incomplete, the argument clearly uses the observed prevalence of pain and hardship to undermine the claim that creation is an act of beneficence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that the world is full of blessings and that most people are happy, but the argument highlights the overwhelming evidence of suffering.
Correction: The passage shows that empirical observation reveals widespread suffering, which contradicts the idea that creation is purely beneficent.

Checkpoint
What does the beginning of the sixth argument say about the conditions of most people in this world?
A) They are happy and content
B) They are in griefs, worries, and fears
C) They have everything they desire
D) They are independent of needs

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument uses the observable prevalence of suffering in this world to refute the claim that creation is an act of beneficence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read al-Razi's list of sufferings—griefs, worries, fears, calamities—which of these have you personally experienced or witnessed in someone close to you? How did that experience affect your belief in God's goodness?
?
The passage contrasts the assumption that all desires are fulfilled with the reality of widespread pain. Have you ever felt that God’s creation is not beneficent because of the suffering you see? How do you respond to that feeling without dismissing the evidence?
?
If you were to honestly observe the conditions of most people in the world today, would you conclude that the world is designed for comfort or for hardship? How does your answer affect your gratitude and trust in God?
